2x" data-file-width="457" data-file-height="336" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Constituencies of Iceland</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 165.33333333333px"><div style="width: 165.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 163.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png/245px-Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png" width="164" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png/367px-Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png/489px-Sveitarf%C3%A9l%C3%B6g-landsv%C3%A6%C3%B0i.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="589" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Iceland" title="Municipalities of Iceland">Municipalities of Iceland</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n</center>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg/220px-Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg/330px-Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg/440px-Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1332" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nordic_ministers_whitehouse_2016.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nordic prime ministers and the president of Finland visiting the White House in 2016, with Iceland\'s <a href="/wiki/Sigur%C3%B0ur_Ingi_J%C3%B3hannsson" title="Sigur\xc3\xb0ur Ingi J\xc3\xb3hannsson">Sigur\xc3\xb0ur</a> second from the left.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Iceland" title="Foreign relations of Iceland">Foreign relations of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accession_of_Iceland_to_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Accession of Iceland to the European Union">Accession of Iceland to the European Union</a></div>\n<p>Iceland, which is a member of the <a href="/wiki/UN" class="mw-redirect" title="UN">UN</a>, <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">EFTA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>, maintains diplomatic and commercial relations with practically all nations, but its ties with the Nordic countries, Germany, the United States, Canada and the other NATO nations are particularly close. Historically, due to cultural, economic and linguistic similarities, Iceland is a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic country">Nordic country</a>, and it participates in intergovernmental cooperation through the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council" title="Nordic Council">Nordic Council</a>.\n</p><p>Iceland is a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> (EEA), which allows the country access to the single market of the European Union (EU). It was not a member of the EU, but in July 2009 the Icelandic parliament, the Althing, voted in favour of application for EU membership<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and officially applied on 17 July 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> However, in 2013, opinion polls showed that many Icelanders were now against joining the EU; following <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_parliamentary_election,_2013" title="Icelandic parliamentary election, 2013">2013 elections</a> the two parties that formed the island\'s new government\xe2\x80\x94the centrist Progressive Party and the right-wing Independence Party\xe2\x80\x94announced they would hold a referendum on EU membership.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Iceland" title="Military of Iceland">Military of Iceland</a></div>\n<p>Iceland has no <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Coast_Guard" title="Icelandic Coast Guard">Icelandic Coast Guard</a> which also maintains the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Air_Defence_System" title="Iceland Air Defence System">Iceland Air Defence System</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Crisis_Response_Unit" title="Iceland Crisis Response Unit">Iceland Crisis Response Unit</a> to support peacekeeping missions, perform paramilitary functions.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Defense_Force" title="Iceland Defense Force">Iceland Defense Force (IDF)</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> <a href="/wiki/Command_(military_formation)" title="Command (military formation)">command</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">United States Armed Forces</a> from 1951 to 2006. The IDF, created at the request of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, came into existence when the United States signed an agreement to provide for the defense of Iceland. The IDF also consisted of civilian Icelanders and military members of other NATO nations. The IDF was downsized after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> maintained four to six <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">interceptor aircraft</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Keflavik" title="Naval Air Station Keflavik">Naval Air Station Keflavik</a>, until they were withdrawn on 30 September 2006. Since May 2008, NATO nations have periodically deployed fighters to patrol Icelandic airspace under the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Air_Policing" title="Icelandic Air Policing">Icelandic Air Policing</a> mission.<sup id="cite_ref-French_Air_Force_in_Iceland_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-French_Air_Force_in_Iceland-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NATO_ACO_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NATO_ACO-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Iceland supported the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a> despite much domestic controversy, deploying a <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Coast_Guard" title="Icelandic Coast Guard">Coast Guard</a> <a href="/wiki/Explosive_Ordnance_Disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Explosive Ordnance Disposal">EOD</a> team to Iraq,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> which was replaced later by members of the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Crisis_Response_Unit" title="Iceland Crisis Response Unit">Iceland Crisis Response Unit</a>. Iceland has also participated in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">conflict in Afghanistan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1999_NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" class="mw-redirect" title="1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia</a>. Despite the ongoing financial crisis the first <a href="/wiki/ICGV_%C3%9E%C3%B3r" title="ICGV \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r">new patrol ship</a> in decades was launched on 29 April 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Iceland was the neutral host of the historic 1986 <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Summit" title="Reykjav\xc3\xadk Summit">Reagan\xe2\x80\x93Gorbachev summit</a> in Reykjav\xc3\xadk, which set the stage for the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Iceland\'s principal historical international disputes involved disagreements over <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">fishing rights</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Conflict with the United Kingdom led to a series of so-called <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a>, which included confrontations between the Icelandic Coast Guard and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> over British fishermen, in 1952\xe2\x80\x931956 due to the extension of Iceland\'s fishing zone from 3 to 4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nmi</a> (5.6 to 7.4&#160;km; 3.5 to 4.6&#160;mi), 1958\xe2\x80\x931961 following a further extension to 12&#160;nmi (22.2&#160;km; 13.8&#160;mi), 1972\xe2\x80\x931973 with another extension to 50&#160;nmi (92.6&#160;km; 57.5&#160;mi); and in 1975\xe2\x80\x931976 another extension to 200&#160;nmi (370.4&#160;km; 230.2&#160;mi).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>, Iceland is the most peaceful country in the world, due to its lack of armed forces, low crime rate, and high level of socio-political stability.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Iceland is listed in the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a> book as the "country ranked most at peace" and the "lowest military spending per capita".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Akureyri.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Akureyri.JPG/220px-Akureyri.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Akureyri.JPG/330px-Akureyri.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Akureyri.JPG/440px-Akureyri.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Akureyri.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> is the largest town in Iceland outside the <a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Capital Region</a>. Most rural towns are based on the fishing industry, which provides 40% of Iceland\'s exports</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Iceland" title="Economy of Iceland">Economy of Iceland</a></div>\n<p>In 2007, Iceland was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">seventh most productive country in the world per capita</a> (US$54,858), and the fifth most productive by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP</a> at <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> ($40,112). About 85 percent of total <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> supply in Iceland is derived from domestically produced renewable energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Utilization of abundant <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a> has made Iceland the world\'s largest electricity producer per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> As a result of its commitment to renewable energy, the 2016 Global Green Economy Index ranked Iceland among the top 10 greenest economies in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-ggei_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggei-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\nHistorically, Iceland\'s economy depended heavily on fishing, which still provides 40% of export earnings and employs 7% of the work force.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_59-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The economy is vulnerable to declining fish stocks and drops in world prices for its main material exports: fish and fish products, aluminium, and <a href="/wiki/Ferrosilicon" title="Ferrosilicon">ferrosilicon</a>. <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_Iceland" title="Whaling in Iceland">Whaling in Iceland</a> has been historically significant. Iceland still relies heavily on fishing, but its importance is diminishing from an export share of 90% in the 1960s to 40% in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until the 20th century, Iceland was among the poorest countries in Europe. Currently, it remains one of the most developed countries in the world. Strong economic growth had led Iceland to be ranked <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">first</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> report for 2007/2008,<sup id="cite_ref-HDI_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> although in 2011 its HDI rating had fallen to 14th place as a result of the economic crisis. Nevertheless, according to the Economist Intelligence Index of 2011, Iceland has the 2nd highest quality of life in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Based on the <a href="/wiki/Gini_coefficient" title="Gini coefficient">Gini coefficient</a>, Iceland also has one of the lowest rates of income inequality in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> and when <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_inequality-adjusted_HDI" title="List of countries by inequality-adjusted HDI">adjusted for inequality</a>, its HDI ranking is 6th.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Iceland\'s unemployment rate has declined consistently since the crisis, with 4.8% of the labour force being unemployed as of June 2012, compared to 6% in 2011 and 8.1% in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_59-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many political parties remain opposed to EU membership, primarily due to Icelanders\' concern about losing control over their natural resources (particularly fisheries).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The national currency of Iceland is the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_kr%C3%B3na" title="Icelandic kr\xc3\xb3na">Icelandic kr\xc3\xb3na</a> (ISK). Iceland is the only country in the world to have a population under two million yet still have a floating exchange rate and an independent monetary policy.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A poll released on 5 March 2010 by Capacent Gallup showed that 31% of respondents were in favour of adopting the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> and 69% opposed.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Another Capacent Gallup poll conducted in February 2012 found that 67.4% of Icelanders would reject EU membership in a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg/220px-Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg/330px-Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg/440px-Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2668" data-file-height="2085" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graphical depiction of Iceland\'s product exports in 28 colour-coded categories</div></div></div>\n<p>Iceland\'s economy has been diversifying into manufacturing and service industries in the last decade, including software production, <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, and finance; industry accounts for around a quarter of economic activity, while services comprise close to 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The tourism sector is expanding, especially in <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a> and whale-watching. On average, Iceland receives around 1.1&#160;million visitors annually, which is more than three times the native population.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_\xe2\x80\x93_OECD_Better_Life_Index_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_\xe2\x80\x93_OECD_Better_Life_Index-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> 1.7 million people visited Iceland in 2016, 3 times more than the number that came in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Iceland\'s agriculture industry, accounting for 5.4% of GDP,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_59-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> consists mainly of potatoes, green vegetables (in <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouses</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mutton" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutton">mutton</a> and dairy products.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_59-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The financial centre is <a href="/wiki/Borgart%C3%BAn" title="Borgart\xc3\xban">Borgart\xc3\xban</a> in Reykjav\xc3\xadk, which hosts a large number of companies and three investment banks. Iceland\'s <a href="/wiki/Stock_market" title="Stock market">stock market</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Stock_Exchange" title="Iceland Stock Exchange">Iceland Stock Exchange</a> (ISE), was established in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Iceland is ranked 27th in the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">Index of Economic Freedom</a>, lower than in prior years but still among the freest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> As of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, it ranks 29th in the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>\'s Global Competitive Index, one place lower than in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/INSEAD" title="INSEAD">INSEAD</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index_(Cornell_University,_INSEAD_and_WIPO)" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Innovation Index (Cornell University, INSEAD and WIPO)">Global Innovation Index</a>, Iceland is the 11th most innovative country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Unlike most Western European countries, Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Flat_tax" title="Flat tax">flat tax</a> system: the main personal income tax rate is a flat 22.75%, and combined with municipal taxes, the total tax rate equals no more than 35.7%, not including the many deductions that are available.<sup id="cite_ref-economicfreedom2008_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicfreedom2008-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax" title="Corporate tax">corporate tax</a> rate is a flat 18%, one of the lowest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-economicfreedom2008_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicfreedom2008-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> There is also a <a href="/wiki/Value_added_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Value added tax">value added tax</a>, whereas a net <a href="/wiki/Wealth_tax" title="Wealth tax">wealth tax</a> was eliminated in 2006. Employment regulations are relatively flexible and the labour market is one of the freest in the world. Property rights are strong and Iceland is one of the few countries where they are applied to <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_management" title="Fisheries management">fishery management</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-economicfreedom2008_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicfreedom2008-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Like other <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare states</a>, taxpayers pay various subsidies to each other, but with spending being less than in most European countries.\n</p><p>Despite low tax rates, agricultural assistance is the highest among OECD countries and a potential impediment to structural change. Also, health care and education spending have relatively poor returns by OECD measures, though improvements have been made in both areas. The OECD <i>Economic Survey of Iceland 2008</i> had highlighted Iceland\'s challenges in currency and macroeconomic policy.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> There was a <a href="/wiki/Currency_crisis" title="Currency crisis">currency crisis</a> that started in the spring of 2008, and on 6 October trading in Iceland\'s banks was suspended as the government battled to save the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> An assessment by the OECD 2011<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> determined that Iceland has made progress in many areas, particularly in creating a sustainable fiscal policy and restoring the health of the financial sector; however, challenges remain in making the fishing industry more efficient and sustainable, as well as in improving monetary policy to address inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Iceland\'s public debt has decreased since the economic crisis, and as of  2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> is the 31th highest in the world by proportion of national GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_contraction">Economic contraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economic contraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008\xe2\x80\x932011 Icelandic financial crisis">2008\xe2\x80\x932011 Icelandic financial crisis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:W16_Protesters_2659.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/W16_Protesters_2659.JPG/220px-W16_Protesters_2659.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/W16_Protesters_2659.JPG/330px-W16_Protesters_2659.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/W16_Protesters_2659.JPG/440px-W16_Protesters_2659.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2075" data-file-height="1390" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:W16_Protesters_2659.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/2009_Icelandic_financial_crisis_protests" title="2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests">2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Iceland had been hit especially hard by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> that began in December 2007, because of the failure of its banking system and a subsequent economic crisis. Before the crash of the country\'s three largest banks, <a href="/wiki/Glitnir_(bank)" title="Glitnir (bank)">Glitnir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landsbanki" title="Landsbanki">Landsbanki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaupthing" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaupthing">Kaupthing</a>, their combined debt exceeded approximately six times the nation\'s gross domestic product of \xe2\x82\xac14&#160;billion ($19&#160;billion).<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.1_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.1-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In October 2008, the Icelandic parliament passed emergency legislation to minimise the impact of the financial crisis. The Financial Supervisory Authority of Iceland used permission granted by the emergency legislation to take over the domestic operations of the three largest banks.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Icelandic officials, including central bank governor <a href="/wiki/Dav%C3%AD%C3%B0_Oddsson" title="Dav\xc3\xad\xc3\xb0 Oddsson">Dav\xc3\xad\xc3\xb0 Oddsson</a>, stated that the state did not intend to take over any of the banks\' foreign debts or assets. Instead, new banks were established to take on the domestic operations of the banks, and the old banks will be run into bankruptcy.\n</p><p>On 28 October 2008, the Icelandic government raised interest rates to 18% (as of August 2010, it was 7%), a move forced in part by the terms of acquiring a loan from <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF). After the rate hike, trading on the Icelandic kr\xc3\xb3na finally resumed on the open market, with valuation at around 250 ISK per Euro, less than one-third the value of the 1:70 exchange rate during most of 2008, and a significant drop from the 1:150 exchange ratio of the week before. On 20 November 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> agreed to lend Iceland $2.5&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 26 January 2009, the coalition government collapsed due to the public dissent over the handling of the financial crisis. A new left-wing government was formed a week later and immediately set about removing Central Bank governor Dav\xc3\xad\xc3\xb0 Oddsson and his aides from the bank through changes in law. Dav\xc3\xad\xc3\xb0 was removed on 26 February 2009 in the wake of protests outside the Central Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thousands of Icelanders have moved from the country after the collapse, and many of those moved to <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>. In 2005, 293 people moved from Iceland to Norway; in 2009, the figure was 1,625.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> In April 2010, the Icelandic Parliament\xe2\x80\x98s Special Investigation Commission published the findings of its investigation,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> revealing the extent of control fraud in this crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> By June 2012, Landsbanki managed to repay about half of the <a href="/wiki/Icesave_dispute" title="Icesave dispute">Icesave</a> debt.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a>, Iceland is on the trajectory of 2% unemployment as a result of crisis-management decisions made back in 2008, including allowing the banks to fail.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Route1(iceland).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Route1%28iceland%29.png/220px-Route1%28iceland%29.png" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Route1%28iceland%29.png/330px-Route1%28iceland%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Route1%28iceland%29.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="253" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Route1(iceland).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Route_1_(Iceland)" title="Route 1 (Iceland)">The Ring Road of Iceland</a> and some towns it passes through: 1. <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjav\xc3\xadk">Reykjav\xc3\xadk</a>, 2. <a href="/wiki/Borgarnes" title="Borgarnes">Borgarnes</a>, 3. <a href="/wiki/Bl%C3%B6ndu%C3%B3s" title="Bl\xc3\xb6ndu\xc3\xb3s">Bl\xc3\xb6ndu\xc3\xb3s</a>, 4. <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a>, 5. <a href="/wiki/Egilssta%C3%B0ir" title="Egilssta\xc3\xb0ir">Egilssta\xc3\xb0ir</a>, 6. <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B6fn" title="H\xc3\xb6fn">H\xc3\xb6fn</a>, 7. <a href="/wiki/Selfoss_(town)" title="Selfoss (town)">Selfoss</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Iceland" title="Transport in Iceland">Transport in Iceland</a></div>\n<p>Iceland has a high level of car ownership per capita; with a car for every 1.5 inhabitants; it is the main form of transport.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Iceland has 13,034&#160;km (8,099&#160;mi) of administered roads, of which 4,617&#160;km (2,869&#160;mi) are paved and 8,338&#160;km (5,181&#160;mi) are not. A great number of roads remain unpaved, mostly little-used rural roads. The road speed limits are 30&#160;km/h (19&#160;mph) and 50&#160;km/h (31&#160;mph) in towns, 80&#160;km/h (50&#160;mph) on gravel country roads and 90&#160;km/h (56&#160;mph) on hard-surfaced roads.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Route_1_(Iceland)" title="Route 1 (Iceland)">Route 1</a>, or the Ring Road (Icelandic: <i>\xc3\x9ej\xc3\xb3\xc3\xb0vegur 1</i> or <i>Hringvegur</i>), was completed in 1974, and is a main road that runs around Iceland and connects all the inhabited parts of the island, with the interior of the island being uninhabited. This paved road is 1,332&#160;km (828&#160;mi)<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> long with one lane in each direction, except near larger towns and cities and in the <a href="/wiki/Hvalfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur_Tunnel" title="Hvalfj\xc3\xb6r\xc3\xb0ur Tunnel">Hvalfj\xc3\xb6r\xc3\xb0ur Tunnel</a> (also the site of a toll) where it has more lanes. Many bridges on it, especially in the north and east, are single lane and made of timber and/or steel.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Keflav%C3%ADk_International_Airport" title="Keflav\xc3\xadk International Airport">Keflav\xc3\xadk International Airport</a> (KEF) <sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> is the largest airport and the main aviation hub for international passenger transport. It serves several international and domestic airline companies.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> KEF is in the vicinity of the larger metropolitan capital areas, 49&#160;km (30&#160;mi) <sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> to the WSW of Reykjav\xc3\xadk center, reachable only by bus services<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> and passenger cars. Iceland has no passenger railways.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Airport" title="Reykjav\xc3\xadk Airport">Reykjav\xc3\xadk Airport</a> (RKV) <sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> is the second largest airport located just 1,5&#160;km from the capital centre. RKV serves general aviation traffic and has daily- or regular domestic flights to 12 local townships within Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> RKV also serves international flights to <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, business and private airplanes along with aviation training.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Akureyri_Airport" title="Akureyri Airport">Akureyri Airport</a> (AEY) <sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Egilssta%C3%B0ir_Airport" title="Egilssta\xc3\xb0ir Airport">Egilssta\xc3\xb0ir Airport</a> (EGS) <sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> are two other domestic airports with limited international service capacity. There are a total of 103 registered airports and airfields in Iceland; most of them are unpaved and located in rural areas. The second longest runway is at Geitamelur, a four-runway glider field around 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) east of Reykjav\xc3\xadk.\n</p><p>Six main ferry services provide regular access to various outpost communities or shorten travel distances.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="per WP:CIRCULAR (October 2017)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Iceland" title="Renewable energy in Iceland">Renewable energy in Iceland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/220px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/330px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/440px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station</a> services the <a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Capital Region</a>\'s hot water and electricity needs. Virtually all of Iceland\'s electricity comes from renewable resources.<sup id="cite_ref-energy_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energy-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable sources</a>\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>\xe2\x80\x94provide effectively all of Iceland\'s electricity<sup id="cite_ref-energy_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energy-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and around 85% of the nation\'s total primary energy consumption,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> with most of the remainder consisting of imported oil products used in transportation and in the fishing fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-statice1_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statice1-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Iceland expects to be energy-independent by 2050. Iceland\'s largest geothermal power plants are <a href="/wiki/Hellishei%C3%B0i_Power_Station" title="Hellishei\xc3\xb0i Power Station">Hellishei\xc3\xb0i</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rahnj%C3%BAkar_Hydropower_Plant" title="K\xc3\xa1rahnj\xc3\xbakar Hydropower Plant">K\xc3\xa1rahnj\xc3\xbakar Hydropower Plant</a> is the country\'s largest hydroelectric power station.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> When the K\xc3\xa1rahnj\xc3\xbakavirkjun started operating, Iceland became the world\'s largest electricity producer per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Icelanders emit 6.29&#160;tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> in 2009 equivalent of greenhouse gases per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Iceland is one of the few countries that have filling stations dispensing hydrogen fuel for cars powered by <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a>. It is also one of a few countries currently capable of producing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in adequate quantities at a reasonable cost, because of Iceland\'s plentiful renewable sources of energy.\n</p><p>On 22 January 2009, Iceland announced its first round of <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">offshore</a> licences for companies wanting to conduct <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon_exploration" title="Hydrocarbon exploration">hydrocarbon exploration</a> and production in a region northeast of Iceland, known as the <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen_Microcontinent#Economic_resources" title="Jan Mayen Microcontinent">Dreki area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Two exploration licenses have been awarded.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2010 Iceland was noted by Guinness World Records as "the Greenest Country", reaching highest score by the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Sustainability_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental Sustainability Index">Environmental Sustainability Index</a> which measures a country\'s water use, biodiversity and adoption of clean energies with a score of 93.5/100.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of  2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the government of Iceland is in talks with the government of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> about the possibility of constructing a <a href="/wiki/High-voltage_direct_current" title="High-voltage direct current">high-voltage direct-current</a> connector for transmission of electricity between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Such a cable would give Iceland access to a market where electricity prices have generally been much higher than those in Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Iceland has considerable renewable energy resources, especially geothermal energy and hydropower resources,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> and most of the potential has not been developed, partly because there is not enough demand for additional electricity generation capacity from the residents and industry of Iceland, but the United Kingdom is interested in importing inexpensive electricity from renewable sources of energy, and this could lead to further development of the energy resources.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_science">Education and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Education and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Iceland" title="Education in Iceland">Education in Iceland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_(main_building,_2004).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg/220px-Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg/330px-Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg/440px-Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_(main_building,_2004).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reykjav\xc3\xadk Junior College (<i><a href="/wiki/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Menntask\xc3\xb3linn \xc3\xad Reykjav\xc3\xadk">Menntask\xc3\xb3linn \xc3\xad Reykjav\xc3\xadk</a></i>), located in downtown Reykjav\xc3\xadk, is the oldest <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a> in Iceland</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education,_Science_and_Culture_(Iceland)" title="Ministry of Education, Science and Culture (Iceland)">Ministry of Education, Science and Culture</a> is responsible for the policies and methods that schools must use, and they issue the National Curriculum Guidelines. However, playschools, primary schools, and lower secondary schools are funded and administered by the municipalities. The government does allow citizens to <a href="/wiki/Homeschooling" title="Homeschooling">Home educate</a> their children, however under a very strict set of demands.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Students must adhere closely to the government mandated curriculum, and the parent teaching must acquire a government approved teaching certificate.\n</p><p>Nursery school, or <i>leiksk\xc3\xb3li</i>, is non-compulsory education for children younger than six years, and is the first step in the education system. The current legislation concerning playschools was passed in 1994. They are also responsible for ensuring that the curriculum is suitable so as to make the transition into compulsory education as easy as possible.\n</p><p>Compulsory education, or <i>grunnsk\xc3\xb3li</i>, comprises primary and lower secondary education, which often is conducted at the same institution. Education is mandatory by law for children aged from 6 to 16&#160;years. The school year lasts nine months, beginning between 21 August and 1 September, ending between 31 May and 10 June. The minimum number of school days was once 170, but after a new teachers\' wage contract, it increased to 180. Lessons take place five days a week. All public schools have mandatory education in Christianity, although an exemption may be considered by the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Education,_Science_and_Culture_(Iceland)" title="Minister of Education, Science and Culture (Iceland)">Minister of Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Fox_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Fox-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upper secondary education, or <i>framhaldssk\xc3\xb3li</i>, follows lower secondary education. These schools are also known as <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasia</a> in English. Though not compulsory, everyone who has had a compulsory education has the right to upper secondary education. This stage of education is governed by the Upper Secondary School Act of 1996. All schools in Iceland are mixed sex schools. The largest seat of higher education is the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Iceland" title="University of Iceland">University of Iceland</a>, which has its main campus in central Reykjav\xc3\xadk. Other schools offering university-level instruction include <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_University" title="Reykjav\xc3\xadk University">Reykjav\xc3\xadk University</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Akureyri" title="University of Akureyri">University of Akureyri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_University_of_Iceland" title="Agricultural University of Iceland">Agricultural University of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bifr%C3%B6st_University" title="Bifr\xc3\xb6st University">Bifr\xc3\xb6st University</a>.\n</p><p>An OECD assessment found 64% of Icelanders aged 25\xe2\x80\x9364 have earned the equivalent of a high-school degree, which is lower than the OECD average of 73%. Among 25- to 34-year-olds, only 69% have earned the equivalent of a high-school degree, significantly lower than the OECD average of 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_\xe2\x80\x93_OECD_Better_Life_Index_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_\xe2\x80\x93_OECD_Better_Life_Index-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Iceland\'s education system is considered excellent: the <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a> currently ranks it as the 16th best performing, above the OECD average.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Students were particularly proficient in reading and mathematics.\n</p><p>According to a 2013 <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> report by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, Iceland spends around 3.11% of its GDP on scientific research and development (R&amp;D), over 1 percentage point higher than the EU average of 2.03%, and has set a target of 4% to reach by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> A 2010 <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> report found that out of 72 countries that spend the most on R&amp;D (100&#160;million US dollars or more), Iceland ranked 9th by proportion of GDP, tied with Taiwan, Switzerland, and Germany and ahead of France, the UK, and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For statistics on demographics, see <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Iceland" title="Demographics of Iceland">Demographics of Iceland</a>.</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelanders</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG/220px-Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG/330px-Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG/440px-Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3296" data-file-height="2472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjav\xc3\xadk">Reykjav\xc3\xadk</a>, Iceland\'s largest metropolitan area and the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Capital Region</a> which, with a population of 212,385, makes for 63% of Iceland\'s population. (numbers from 2016)</div></div></div>\n<p>The original population of Iceland was of <a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> origin. This is evident from literary evidence dating from the settlement period as well as from later scientific studies such as <a href="/wiki/Blood_type" title="Blood type">blood type</a> and genetic analyses. One such genetic study indicated that the majority of the male settlers were of Nordic origin while the majority of the women were of Gaelic origin, meaning many settlers of Iceland were Norsemen who brought Gaelic slaves with them.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Iceland has extensive genealogical records dating back to the late 17th century and fragmentary records extending back to the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">Age of Settlement</a>. The biopharmaceutical company <a href="/wiki/DeCODE_genetics" title="DeCODE genetics">deCODE genetics</a> has funded the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> database that is intended to cover all of Iceland\'s known inhabitants. It views the database, called <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%8Dslendingab%C3%B3k_(genealogical_database)" title="\xc3\x8dslendingab\xc3\xb3k (genealogical database)">\xc3\x8dslendingab\xc3\xb3k</a></i>, as a valuable tool for conducting research on genetic diseases, given the relative isolation of Iceland\'s population.\n</p><p>The population of the island is believed to have varied from 40,000 to 60,000 in the period ranging from initial settlement until the mid-19th century. During that time, cold winters, ash fall from volcanic eruptions, and <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plagues</a> adversely affected the population several times.<sup id="cite_ref-tomasson_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tomasson-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> There were 37 <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> years in Iceland between 1500 and 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> The first census was carried out in 1703 and revealed that the population was then 50,358. After the destructive volcanic eruptions of the <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki volcano</a> during 1783\xe2\x80\x931784, the population reached a low of about 40,000.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Improving living conditions have triggered a rapid increase in population since the mid-19th century\xe2\x80\x94from about 60,000 in 1850 to 320,000 in 2008. Iceland has a relatively young population for a developed country, with one out of five people being 14&#160;years old or younger. With a fertility rate of 2.1, Iceland is one of only a few European countries with a birth rate <a href="/wiki/Replacement_fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement fertility rate">sufficient for long-term population growth</a> (see table on the below).<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="font-size: 98%;">\n<table class="wikitable" style="clear: left; right: left; margin-right: 1em;">\n<caption>Population projection <br />(1 January)<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Year\n</th>\n<th>Low\n</th>\n<th>Medium\n</th>\n<th>High\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2014\n</td>\n<td colspan="3">325,671\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2015\n</td>\n<td colspan="3">332,529\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2020\n</td>\n<td>340,418\n</td>\n<td>342,716\n</td>\n<td>346,279\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2025\n</td>\n<td>352,280\n</td>\n<td>357,894\n</td>\n<td>365,893\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2030\n</td>\n<td>361,853\n</td>\n<td>371,796\n</td>\n<td>385,405\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2035\n</td>\n<td>369,888\n</td>\n<td>384,397\n</td>\n<td>404,053\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2040\n</td>\n<td>376,580\n</td>\n<td>395,866\n</td>\n<td>422,047\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2045\n</td>\n<td>381,846\n</td>\n<td>406,271\n</td>\n<td>439,756\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2050\n</td>\n<td>385,536\n</td>\n<td>415,627\n</td>\n<td>457,317\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2055\n</td>\n<td>387,489\n</td>\n<td>423,790\n</td>\n<td>474,561\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align: center;">\n<td>2060\n</td>\n<td>387,597\n</td>\n<td>430,545\n</td>\n<td>490,976\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<p>In December 2007, 33,678 people (13.5% of the total population) living in Iceland had been born abroad, including children of Icelandic parents living abroad. Around 19,000 people (6% of the population) held foreign citizenship. <a href="/wiki/Polish_diaspora" title="Polish diaspora">Polish</a> people make up the largest minority group by a considerable margin, and still form the bulk of the foreign workforce. About 8,000 Poles now live in Iceland, 1,500 of them in <a href="/wiki/Fjar%C3%B0abygg%C3%B0" title="Fjar\xc3\xb0abygg\xc3\xb0">Fjar\xc3\xb0abygg\xc3\xb0</a> where they make up 75% of the workforce who are constructing the Fjar\xc3\xb0ar\xc3\xa1l aluminium plant.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The recent increase in <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> has been credited<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2014)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> to a <a href="/wiki/Labour_shortage" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour shortage">labour shortage</a> due to the booming economy at the time, as well as to the lifting of restrictions on the movement of people from the countries that were a part of the <a href="/wiki/2004_enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="2004 enlargement of the European Union">2004 enlargement of the European Union</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Large-scale construction projects in the east of Iceland (see <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rahnj%C3%BAkar_Hydropower_Plant" title="K\xc3\xa1rahnj\xc3\xbakar Hydropower Plant">K\xc3\xa1rahnj\xc3\xbakar Hydropower Plant</a>) have also brought in many people whose stay is expected to be temporary. Many Polish immigrants were also considering leaving in 2008 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008\xe2\x80\x932011 Icelandic financial crisis">Icelandic financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The southwest corner of Iceland is the most densely populated region. It is also the location of the capital Reykjav\xc3\xadk, the northernmost national capital in the world. The largest towns outside the Greater Reykjav\xc3\xadk area are <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reykjanesb%C3%A6r" title="Reykjanesb\xc3\xa6r">Reykjanesb\xc3\xa6r</a>, although the latter is relatively close to the capital.\n</p><p>Some 500 Icelanders under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Erik_the_Red" title="Erik the Red">Erik the Red</a> colonised <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> in the late 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The total population reached a high point of perhaps 5,000 and developed independent institutions before disappearing by 1500.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> People from Greenland attempted to set up a colony at <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a> in North America, but abandoned it in the face of hostility from the indigenous residents.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Emigration of Icelanders to the United States and Canada began in the 1870s. As of  2006<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Canada had over 88,000 people of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic Canadian">Icelandic</a> descent,<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> while there are more than 40,000 Americans of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic American">Icelandic</a> descent, according to the 2000 US census.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanisation">Urbanisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Urbanisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Iceland\'s 10 most populous urban areas:\n</p>\n<table style="margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 100%; border: 1px solid darkgray; background: #f9f9f9">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="10" style="padding:0.3em style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Iceland" title="Languages of Iceland">Languages of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic language</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_name" title="Icelandic name">Icelandic name</a></div>\n<p>Iceland\'s official written and spoken language is <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic language</a> descended from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>. In grammar and vocabulary, it has changed less from Old Norse than the other Nordic languages; Icelandic has preserved more verb and noun <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, and has to a considerable extent developed new vocabulary based on native roots rather than borrowings from other languages. The puristic tendency in the development of Icelandic vocabulary is to a large degree a result of conscious language planning, in addition to centuries of isolation. Icelandic is the only living language to retain the use of the <a href="/wiki/Runic" class="mw-redirect" title="Runic">runic</a> letter <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x9e">\xc3\x9e</a> in <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>. The closest living relative of the Icelandic language is <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Sign_Language" title="Icelandic Sign Language">Icelandic Sign Language</a> was officially recognised as a minority language in 2011. In education, its use for Iceland\'s deaf community is <a href="/wiki/Legal_recognition_of_sign_languages" title="Legal recognition of sign languages">regulated</a> by the <i>National Curriculum Guide</i>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> are compulsory subjects in the school curriculum. English is widely understood and spoken, while basic to moderate knowledge of Danish is common mainly among the older generations.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> is mostly spoken by the local Polish community (the largest minority of Iceland), and Danish is mostly spoken in a way largely comprehensible to Swedes and Norwegians\xe2\x80\x94it is often referred to as <i>skandinav\xc3\xadska</i> (i. e. <i>Scandinavian</i>) in Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rather than using family names, as is the usual custom in most Western nations, Icelanders carry <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Matronymic" title="Matronymic">matronymic</a> surnames, patronyms being far more commonly practiced. Patronymic last names are based on the first name of the father, while matronymic names are based on the first name of the mother. These follow the person\'s given name, e.g. <i>El\xc3\xadsabet J\xc3\xb3nsd\xc3\xb3ttir</i> ("El\xc3\xadsabet, J\xc3\xb3n\'s daughter" (J\xc3\xb3n, being the father)) or <i>\xc3\x93lafur Katr\xc3\xadnarson</i> ("\xc3\x93lafur, Katr\xc3\xadn\'s son" (Katr\xc3\xadn being the mother)).<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, Icelanders refer to one another by their given name, and the Icelandic telephone directory is listed alphabetically by first name rather than by surname.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> All new names must be approved by the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Naming_Committee" title="Icelandic Naming Committee">Icelandic Naming Committee</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> system that is administered by its Ministry of Welfare (<a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>: <i lang="is">Velfer\xc3\xb0arr\xc3\xa1\xc3\xb0uneyti\xc3\xb0</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> and paid for mostly by taxes (85%) and to a lesser extent by service fees (15%). Unlike most countries, there are no private hospitals, and private insurance is practically nonexistent.<sup id="cite_ref-j1_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-j1-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A considerable portion of the government budget is assigned to health care,<sup id="cite_ref-j1_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-j1-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> and Iceland ranks 11th in health care expenditures as a percentage of GDP<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> and 14th in spending per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Overall, the country\'s health care system is one of the best performing in the world, ranked 15th by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> According to an OECD report, Iceland devotes far more resources to healthcare than most industrialised nations. As of  2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Iceland had 3.7 doctors per 1,000 people (compared with an average of 3.1 in OECD countries) and 15.3 nurses per 1,000 people (compared with an OECD average of 8.4).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Icelanders are among the world\'s healthiest people, with 81% reporting they are in good health, according to an OECD survey.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_\xe2\x80\x93_OECD_Better_Life_Index_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_\xe2\x80\x93_OECD_Better_Life_Index-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Although it is a growing problem, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> is not as prevalent as in other <a href="/wiki/Developed_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Iceland has many campaigns for health and wellbeing, including the famous television show <a href="/wiki/LazyTown" title="LazyTown">Lazytown</a>, starring and created by former gymnastics champion <a href="/wiki/Magn%C3%BAs_Scheving" title="Magn\xc3\xbas Scheving">Magnus Scheving</a>. <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> is one of the lowest in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> and the proportion of the population that smokes is lower than the OECD average.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_200-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Almost all women choose to terminate pregnancies of children with <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">Down syndrome</a> in Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The average <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> is 81.8 (compared to an OECD average of 79.5), the 4th highest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Additionally, Iceland has a very low level of pollution, thanks to an overwhelming reliance on cleaner geothermal energy, a low population density, and a high level of environmental consciousness among <a href="/wiki/Citizens" class="mw-redirect" title="Citizens">citizens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> According to an OECD assessment, the amount of toxic materials in the atmosphere is far lower than in any other industrialised country measured.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sta%C3%B0arbakkakirkja.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Sta%C3%B0arbakkakirkja.jpg/220px-Sta%C3%B0arbakkakirkja.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage"
